<li><a href="/wiki/File:A_coloured_voting_box.svg" class="image"><img alt="A coloured voting box.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/A_coloured_voting_box.svg/16px-A_coloured_voting_box.svg.png" decoding="async" width="16" height="16" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/A_coloured_voting_box.svg/24px-A_coloured_voting_box.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/A_coloured_voting_box.svg/32px-A_coloured_voting_box.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Politics" title="Portal:Politics">Politics&#32;portal</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg" class="image"><img alt="Flag of the United Kingdom.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/16px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png" decoding="async" width="16" height="8" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/24px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/32px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="600" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:United_Kingdom" title="Portal:United Kingdom">United Kingdom&#32;portal</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Thatcherism" title="Template:Thatcherism"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Thatcherism" title="Template talk:Thatcherism"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Thatcherism&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Loss of power weakened Heath's control over the party and <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a> deposed him in the <a href="/wiki/1975_Conservative_Party_leadership_election" title="1975 Conservative Party leadership election">1975 leadership election</a>. The UK in the 1970s had seen sustained high <a href="/wiki/Inflation" title="Inflation">inflation</a> rates, which were above 20% at the time of the leadership election, subsequently falling to below 10%; unemployment had risen, and over the winter of 1978–79 there was a series of strikes known as the "<a href="/wiki/Winter_of_Discontent" title="Winter of Discontent">Winter of Discontent</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Thatcher led her party to victory at the <a href="/wiki/1979_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1979 United Kingdom general election">1979 general election</a> with a manifesto which concentrated on the party's philosophy rather than presenting a "shopping list" of policies.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As Prime Minister, Thatcher focused on rejecting the mild liberalism of the <a href="/wiki/Post-war_consensus" title="Post-war consensus">post-war consensus</a> that tolerated or encouraged nationalisation, strong labour unions, heavy regulation, high taxes, and a generous welfare state.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>  She did not challenge the <a href="/wiki/National_Health_Service" title="National Health Service">National Health Service</a>, and supported the Cold War policies of the consensus, but otherwise tried to dismantle and delegitimise it. To replace the old post-war consensus, she built a right-wing political ideology that became known as <a href="/wiki/Thatcherism" title="Thatcherism">Thatcherism</a>, based on social and economic ideas from British and American intellectuals such as <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Hayek" title="Friedrich Hayek">Friedrich Hayek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Milton_Friedman" title="Milton Friedman">Milton Friedman</a>. Thatcher believed that too much socially democratic-oriented government policy was leading to a long-term decline in the British economy. As a result, her government pursued a programme of <a href="/wiki/Economic_liberalism" title="Economic liberalism">economic liberalism</a>, adopting a free-market approach to public services based on the sale of publicly owned industries and utilities, as well as a reduction in trade union power. She held the belief that the existing trend of unions was bringing economic progress to a standstill by enforcing "wildcat" strikes, keeping wages artificially high and forcing unprofitable industries to stay open.
</p><p>One of Thatcher's largest and most successful policies assisted council house tenants in public housing to purchase their homes at favourable rates. The "Right to Buy" had emerged in the late-1940s but was too great a challenge to the <a href="/wiki/Post-War_Consensus" class="mw-redirect" title="Post-War Consensus">Post-War Consensus</a> to win Conservative endorsement. Thatcher from her earliest days in politics favoured the idea because it would lead to a "property-owning democracy", an important idea that had emerged in the 1920s.<sup id="cite_ref-Matthew_Francis_2011_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Matthew_Francis_2011-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Some local Conservative-run councils enacted profitable local sales schemes during the late-1960s. By the 1970s, many working-class people had ample incomes to afford to buy homes, and eagerly adopted Thatcher's invitation to purchase their homes at a sizable discount. The new owners were more likely to vote Conservative, as Thatcher had hoped.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thatcher led the Conservatives to two further electoral victories with landslide<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="vague (April 2017)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> majorities in <a href="/wiki/1983_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1983 United Kingdom general election">1983</a> and <a href="/wiki/1987_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1987 United Kingdom general election">1987</a>. She was greatly admired by her supporters for her leadership in the <a href="/wiki/Falklands_War" title="Falklands War">Falklands War</a> of 1982—which coincided with a dramatic boost in her popularity—and for policies such as giving the right to council house tenants to buy their council house at a discount on market value. She was also deeply unpopular in certain sections of society due to high unemployment, which reached its highest level since the 1930s, peaking at over 3,000,000 people following her economic reforms, and her response to the <a href="/wiki/UK_miners%27_strike_(1984%E2%80%931985)" class="mw-redirect" title="UK miners&#39; strike (1984–1985)">miners' strike</a>. Unemployment had doubled between 1979 and 1982, largely due to Thatcher's <a href="/wiki/Monetarism" title="Monetarism">monetarist</a> battle against inflation.<sup id="cite_ref-news.bbc.co.uk_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.bbc.co.uk-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>  At the time of the <a href="/wiki/1979_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1979 United Kingdom general election">1979 general election</a>, inflation had been at 9% or under for the previous year, having decreased under Callaghan, then increased to over 20% in the first two years of the Thatcher ministry, but it had fallen again to 5.8% by the start of 1983 (it continued to be under 7% until 1990).<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>  The British economy benefitted in the first Thatcher ministry by tax income from <a href="/wiki/North_Sea_oil" title="North Sea oil">North Sea oil</a> coming on stream.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The period of unpopularity of the Conservatives in the early-1980s coincided with a crisis in the Labour Party which then formed the main opposition. The <a href="/wiki/Social_Democratic_Party_(UK)" title="Social Democratic Party (UK)">Social Democratic Party</a> (SDP) was established in 1981 and consisted of more than twenty breakaway Labour MPs, who quickly formed the <a href="/wiki/SDP-Liberal_Alliance" class="mw-redirect" title="SDP-Liberal Alliance">SDP-Liberal Alliance</a> with the Liberal Party. By the turn of 1982, the SDP-Liberal Alliance was ahead of the Conservatives in the <a href="/wiki/Opinion_poll" title="Opinion poll">opinion polls</a>, but victory in the <a href="/wiki/Falklands_War" title="Falklands War">Falklands War</a> in June that year, along with the recovering British economy, saw the Conservatives returning quickly to the top of the opinion polls and winning the 1983 general election with a landslide majority, due to a split opposition vote.<sup id="cite_ref-news.bbc.co.uk_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.bbc.co.uk-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thatcher now faced, arguably, her most serious rival yet after the 1983 general election, when <a href="/wiki/Michael_Foot" title="Michael Foot">Michael Foot</a> resigned as <a href="/wiki/Leader_of_the_Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Leader of the Labour Party (UK)">Leader of the Labour Party</a> and was succeeded by <a href="/wiki/Neil_Kinnock" title="Neil Kinnock">Neil Kinnock</a>. With a new leader at the helm, Labour were clearly determined to defeat the Conservatives at the next election and for virtually the entirety of Thatcher's second ministry it was looking a very serious possibility, as the lead in the opinion polls constantly saw a change in leadership from the Conservatives to Labour, with the Alliance occasionally scraping into first place.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By the time of the general election in June 1987, the economy was stronger, with lower inflation and falling unemployment and Thatcher secured her third successive electoral victory with a second, albeit reduced, landslide majority.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The introduction of the <a href="/wiki/Poll_tax_(Great_Britain)" title="Poll tax (Great Britain)">Community Charge</a> (known by its opponents as the <i><a href="/wiki/Poll_tax_(Great_Britain)" title="Poll tax (Great Britain)">poll tax</a></i>) in 1989 is often cited as contributing to her political downfall. The summer of 1989 saw her fall behind Neil Kinnock's Labour in the opinion polls for the first time since 1986, and her party's fall in popularity continued into 1990. By the second half of that year, opinion polls were showing that Labour had a lead of up to 16 points over the Conservatives and they faced a tough 18 months ahead of them if they were to prevent Kinnock's ambition to become Prime Minister from becoming a reality. At the same time, the economy was sliding into <a href="/wiki/Early_1990s_recession" title="Early 1990s recession">another recession</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Internal party tensions led to a leadership challenge by the Conservative MP <a href="/wiki/Michael_Heseltine" title="Michael Heseltine">Michael Heseltine</a>; and, after months of speculation about her future as Prime Minister, she resigned on 28 November 1990, making way for a new Conservative leader more likely to win the next general election in the interests of party unity.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="John_Major_.281990.E2.80.931997.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="John_Major_(1990–1997)">John Major (1990–1997)</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Major_1996.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/John_Major_1996.jpg/170px-John_Major_1996.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/John_Major_1996.jpg/255px-John_Major_1996.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/John_Major_1996.jpg/340px-John_Major_1996.jpg 2x" data-file-width="470" data-file-height="628" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Major_1996.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/John_Major" title="John Major">John Major</a>, Prime Minister of the United Kingdom (1990–1997)</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/John_Major" title="John Major">John Major</a> won the party leadership election on 27 November 1990, and his appointment led to an almost immediate boost in Conservative Party fortunes. A MORI poll six days before <a href="/wiki/Resignation_of_Margaret_Thatcher" class="mw-redirect" title="Resignation of Margaret Thatcher">Mrs Thatcher's resignation</a> had shown the Conservatives to be 11 points behind Labour, but within two months the Conservatives had returned to the top of the opinion polls with a narrow lead.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_61-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A general election had to be held within the next eighteen months and the UK economy was sliding into <a href="/wiki/Early_1990s" class="mw-redirect" title="Early 1990s">recession</a>, but 1991 was a year of electoral uncertainty as the Conservatives and Labour regularly swapped places at the top of the opinion polls, and Major resisted Neil Kinnock's numerous calls for an immediate election.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_61-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/1992_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1992 United Kingdom general election">election</a> was finally held on 9 April 1992 and the Conservatives won a fourth successive electoral victory, even though the economy was still in recession and most of the polls had predicted either a narrow Labour victory or a <a href="/wiki/Hung_parliament" title="Hung parliament">hung parliament</a>. Major's vigorous campaigning, notably his claim that the UK would have higher prices and higher taxes under a Labour government, was seen to have been crucial in his election win (in which he became the first—and as of 2015, only—Prime Minister to attract 14,000,000 votes in a general election), as was a high-profile campaign by <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)" title="The Sun (United Kingdom)">The Sun</a></i> newspaper against Labour leader Neil Kinnock, who resigned in the aftermath of the election to be succeeded by <a href="/wiki/John_Smith_(Labour_Party_leader)" title="John Smith (Labour Party leader)">John Smith</a>. The Conservative Party also touched upon the issue of immigration, claiming that under Labour, immigration would rise hugely.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The UK economy was deep in recession by this stage and remained so until the end of the year. The <a href="/wiki/Pound_sterling" title="Pound sterling">pound sterling</a> was forced out of the <a href="/wiki/European_Exchange_Rate_Mechanism" title="European Exchange Rate Mechanism">European Exchange Rate Mechanism</a> on 16 September 1992, a day thereafter referred to as <i><a href="/wiki/Black_Wednesday" title="Black Wednesday">Black Wednesday</a></i>.
</p><p>Soon after, approximately one million householders faced repossession of their homes during a recession that saw a sharp rise in unemployment, taking it close to 3,000,000 people. The party subsequently lost much of its reputation for good financial stewardship although the end of the recession was declared in April 1993<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> bringing economic recovery and a fall in unemployment.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rail_modal_share.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Rail_modal_share.png/220px-Rail_modal_share.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Rail_modal_share.png/330px-Rail_modal_share.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Rail_modal_share.png/440px-Rail_modal_share.png 2x" data-file-width="854" data-file-height="567" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rail_modal_share.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rail modal share (rail's share of total travel) 1952–2015<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>From 1994–1997, Major <a href="/wiki/Privatisation_of_British_Rail" title="Privatisation of British Rail">privatised British Rail</a>, splitting it up into <a href="/wiki/Passenger_rail_franchising_in_Great_Britain" title="Passenger rail franchising in Great Britain">franchises to be run by the private sector</a>. Its <a href="/wiki/Impact_of_the_privatisation_of_British_Rail" title="Impact of the privatisation of British Rail">success is hotly debated</a>, with a large increase in passenger numbers and investment in the network balanced by worries about the level of subsidy. Train fares cost more than under <a href="/wiki/British_Rail" title="British Rail">British Rail</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The party was plagued by internal division and infighting, mainly over the UK's role in the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>. The party's <a href="/wiki/Euroscepticism_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Euroscepticism in the United Kingdom">Eurosceptic</a> wing, represented by MPs such as <a href="/wiki/John_Redwood" title="John Redwood">John Redwood</a>, opposed further EU integration, whilst the party's pro-European wing, represented by those such as Chancellor of the Exchequer <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Clarke" title="Kenneth Clarke">Kenneth Clarke</a>, was broadly supportive. The issue of the creation of a single European currency also inflamed tensions, and these would continue to dog the party until the early-2000s (decade). These divisions gave off an impression of a divided party, which had lost touch with the voters.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Major also had to survive a leadership challenge in 1995 by the Secretary of State for Wales, the aforementioned John Redwood. Major survived, but Redwood received 89 votes from MPs, as well as the backing of the <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)" title="The Sun (United Kingdom)">Sun</a></i> newspaper, which described the choice as being between "Redwood or Deadwood". This further undermined Major's influence in the Conservative Party.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Conservative government was also increasingly accused in the media of "<a href="/wiki/Political_scandals_in_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Political scandals in the United Kingdom">sleaze</a>". Their support reached its lowest ebb in late 1994, after the sudden death of Labour Party leader John Smith and the election of <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a> as his successor, when Labour had up to 60% of the vote in opinion polls and had a lead of some 30 points ahead of the Conservatives. The Labour lead was gradually narrowed over the next two years, as the Conservatives gained some credit for the strong economic recovery and fall in unemployment. But as the <a href="/wiki/1997_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1997 United Kingdom general election">1997 general election</a> loomed, despite their high-profile <a href="/wiki/New_Labour,_New_Danger" title="New Labour, New Danger">New Labour, New Danger</a> campaign, it was still looking certain that Labour would win.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_61-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>An effective opposition campaign by the Labour Party culminated in a landslide defeat for the Conservatives in 1997 that was Labour's largest ever parliamentary victory, and the worst defeat for the Conservatives since the <a href="/wiki/1906_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1906 United Kingdom general election">1906 general election</a> 91 years earlier. The <a href="/wiki/1997_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1997 United Kingdom general election">1997 general election</a> left the Conservative Party as an England-only party, with all Scottish and Welsh seats having been lost, and not a single new seat having been gained anywhere.
</p>
<h3><span id="Back_in_opposition_.281997.E2.80.932005.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Back_in_opposition_(1997–2005)">Back in opposition (1997–2005)</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="William_Hague">William Hague</span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/John_Major" title="John Major">John Major</a> resigned as party leader after the Conservatives were heavily defeated in a landslide and was succeeded by <a href="/wiki/William_Hague" title="William Hague">William Hague</a>. Though Hague was a strong orator, a <a href="/wiki/The_Gallup_Organization" class="mw-redirect" title="The Gallup Organization">Gallup poll</a> for <i><a href="/wiki/The_Daily_Telegraph" title="The Daily Telegraph">The Daily Telegraph</a></i> found that two-thirds of voters regarded him as "a bit of a wally",<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> for headlines such as his claim that he drank 14 pints of beer in a single day in his youth. He was also criticised for attending the <a href="/wiki/Notting_Hill_Carnival" title="Notting Hill Carnival">Notting Hill Carnival</a> and for wearing a <a href="/wiki/Baseball_cap" title="Baseball cap">baseball cap</a> in public in what were seen as poor attempts to appeal to younger voters.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Shortly before the <a href="/wiki/2001_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="2001 United Kingdom general election">2001 general election</a>, Hague was much maligned for a speech in which he predicted that a re-elected Labour government would turn the UK into a "foreign land".<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> The BBC also reported that the Conservative peer <a href="/wiki/John_Taylor,_Baron_Taylor_of_Warwick" title="John Taylor, Baron Taylor of Warwick">John Lord Taylor</a> criticised Hague for not removing the whip from <a href="/wiki/John_Townend" title="John Townend">John Townend</a>, a Conservative MP, after the latter made a speech in which he said the British were becoming "a mongrel race", although Hague did reject Townend's views.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/2001_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="2001 United Kingdom general election">2001 general election</a> resulted in a net gain of just one seat for the Conservative Party, just months after the <a href="/wiki/Fuel_protests_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Fuel protests in the United Kingdom">fuel protests of September 2000</a> had seen the Conservatives briefly take a narrow lead over Labour in the opinion polls.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_61-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Having privately set himself a target of 209 seats, matching Labour's performance in <a href="/wiki/1983_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1983 United Kingdom general election">1983</a>—a target which he missed by 43—<a href="/wiki/William_Hague" title="William Hague">William Hague</a> resigned soon after.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Iain_Duncan_Smith_and_Michael_Howard">Iain Duncan Smith and Michael Howard</span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Iain_Duncan_Smith" title="Iain Duncan Smith">Iain Duncan Smith</a> (2001–2003) (often known as IDS or simply: "Duncan Smith") is a strong <a href="/wiki/Euroscepticism" title="Euroscepticism">Eurosceptic</a>, but the issue did not define Duncan Smith's leadership, though during his tenure, Europe ceased to be an issue of division in the party as it united behind calls for a referendum on the proposed <a href="/wiki/Treaty_establishing_a_Constitution_for_Europe" title="Treaty establishing a Constitution for Europe">European Union Constitution</a>.
</p><p>However, before he could lead the party into a general election, Duncan Smith lost the vote on a <a href="/wiki/Motion_of_no_confidence" title="Motion of no confidence">motion of no confidence</a> by MPs who felt that the party would not be returned to government under his leadership. This was despite the Conservative support equalling that of Labour in the months leading up to his departure from the leadership.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_61-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Michael_Howard" title="Michael Howard">Michael Howard</a> then <a href="/wiki/2003_Conservative_Party_leadership_election" title="2003 Conservative Party leadership election">stood for the leadership unopposed</a> on 6 November 2003.
</p><p>Under Howard's leadership in the <a href="/wiki/2005_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="2005 United Kingdom general election">2005 general election</a>, the Conservative Party increased their total vote share by around 0.7% (up to 32.4%) and—more significantly—their number of parliamentary seats by 33 (up to 198 seats). This gain accompanied a large decline in the Labour vote, and the election reduced Labour's majority from 167 to 68 and its share of the vote to 35.2%.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> The campaign, based on the slogan "Are you thinking what we're thinking?", was designed by Australian pollster <a href="/wiki/Lynton_Crosby" title="Lynton Crosby">Lynton Crosby</a>. The day following the election, on 6 May, Howard announced that he did not feel it was right to continue as leader after defeat in the general election, also saying that he would be too old to lead the party into another campaign and would therefore step down after allowing time for the party to amend its leadership election rules.
</p>
<h3><span id="David_Cameron_.282005.E2.80.932016.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="David_Cameron_(2005–2016)">David Cameron (2005–2016)</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Political_positions_of_David_Cameron" title="Political positions of David Cameron">Political positions of David Cameron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Premiership_of_David_Cameron" title="Premiership of David Cameron">Premiership of David Cameron</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Cameron_official.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/David_Cameron_official.jpg/170px-David_Cameron_official.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/David_Cameron_official.jpg/255px-David_Cameron_official.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/David_Cameron_official.jpg/340px-David_Cameron_official.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1440" data-file-height="1920" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Cameron_official.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Prime Minister of the United Kingdom</a> (2010–2016)</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a> won the <a href="/wiki/2005_Conservative_Party_leadership_election" title="2005 Conservative Party leadership election">2005 leadership election</a>. Cameron defeated his closest rival, <a href="/wiki/David_Davis_(British_politician)" title="David Davis (British politician)">David Davis</a>, by a margin of more than two to one, taking 134,446 votes to 64,398. He then announced his intention to reform and realign the Conservatives, saying they needed to change the way they looked, felt, thought and behaved, advocating a more centre-right stance as opposed to their recent staunchly right-wing platform.<sup id="cite_ref-Cameron-speech_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cameron-speech-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Although Cameron's views are probably to the left of the party membership and he sought to make the Conservative brand more attractive to young, socially liberal voters,<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> he has also expressed his admiration for <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a>, describing himself as a "big fan of Thatcher's", though he questions whether that makes him a "Thatcherite". For most of 2006 and the first half of 2007, polls showed leads over Labour for the Conservatives.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Polls became more volatile in summer 2007 with the accession of <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Brown" title="Gordon Brown">Gordon Brown</a> as Prime Minister, although polls gave the Conservatives a lead after October of that year and, by May 2008, with the UK's economy sliding into <a href="/wiki/Great_Recession" title="Great Recession">its first recession since 1992</a>, and a week after local council elections, a <a href="/wiki/YouGov" title="YouGov">YouGov</a> poll commissioned by <a href="/wiki/The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)" title="The Sun (United Kingdom)"><i>The Sun</i> newspaper</a> was published giving the Conservative Party a 26-point lead over Labour, its largest lead since 1968.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> The Conservatives gained control of the London <a href="/wiki/Mayor_of_London" title="Mayor of London">mayoralty</a> for the first time in May 2008 after <a href="/wiki/Boris_Johnson" title="Boris Johnson">Boris Johnson</a> defeated the Labour incumbent, <a href="/wiki/Ken_Livingstone" title="Ken Livingstone">Ken Livingstone</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Conservative lead in the opinion polls had been almost unbroken for nearly three years when Britain finally went to the polls on 6 May 2010, though since the turn of 2010 most polls had shown the Conservative lead as less than 10 points wide. The election resulted in a <a href="/wiki/Hung_parliament" title="Hung parliament">hung parliament</a> with the Conservatives having the most seats (306) but being twenty seats short of an overall majority. Following the resignation of Gordon Brown as Prime Minister and Leader of the Labour Party five days afterwards, David Cameron was named as the country's new Prime Minister and the Conservatives <a href="/wiki/Cameron%E2%80%93Clegg_coalition" title="Cameron–Clegg coalition">entered government in a coalition</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats_(UK)" title="Liberal Democrats (UK)">Liberal Democrats</a>—the first post-war <a href="/wiki/Coalition_government" title="Coalition government">coalition government</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In May 2014, the Conservatives were defeated in the <a href="/wiki/2014_European_Parliament_election_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="2014 European Parliament election in the United Kingdom">European parliamentary elections</a> coming in third place behind the <a href="/wiki/UK_Independence_Party" title="UK Independence Party">UK Independence Party</a> and Labour. UKIP ended with 24 MEPs, Labour 20, and the Conservatives 19. The result was described by UKIP leader <a href="/wiki/Nigel_Farage" title="Nigel Farage">Nigel Farage</a> as "disastrous" for Cameron, and the leaders of the other main parties.<sup id="cite_ref-UKIP_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UKIP-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In September 2014, the Unionist side, championed by Labour as well as by the Conservative Party and the Liberal Democrats, won in the <a href="/wiki/2014_Scottish_independence_referendum" title="2014 Scottish independence referendum">Scottish Independence referendum</a> by 55% No to 45% Yes on the question "Should Scotland be an independent country". This can be seen as a victory for <a href="/wiki/British_Unionism" class="mw-redirect" title="British Unionism">British Unionism</a>, a core part of traditional Conservative ideology, and also for David Cameron as the incumbent Prime Minister.
</p><p>At the <a href="/wiki/2015_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="2015 United Kingdom general election">2015 general election</a>, the Conservatives won a majority of seats in the House of Commons and formed a majority government under David Cameron. The party increased its national vote share, becoming the first incumbent party to do so since 1900. The result was unexpected and exceeded even the party leadership's expectations, as most polls had predicted a hung parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> This was also the first general election since 1992 in which the Conservatives had won an overall majority, although the vote share of 36.9% was lower than the previous four Conservative majority governments under Thatcher and Major.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> In March 2017, the party was fined £70,000, the largest fine of this sort in British political history, after an <a href="/wiki/Electoral_Commission" class="mw-redirect" title="Electoral Commission">Electoral Commission</a> investigation found "significant failures" by the party to report its <a href="/wiki/2015_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="2015 United Kingdom general election">2015 general election</a> campaign spending.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On the morning of Friday 24 June 2016, Cameron announced his intention to resign as Prime Minister, after he failed to convince the British public to <a href="/wiki/EU_Referendum_UK" class="mw-redirect" title="EU Referendum UK">stay in the European Union</a>, and subsequently the <a href="/wiki/2016_Conservative_Party_leadership_election" title="2016 Conservative Party leadership election">Conservative Party leadership election</a> was announced with <a href="/wiki/Theresa_May" title="Theresa May">Theresa May</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Gove" title="Michael Gove">Michael Gove</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Crabb" title="Stephen Crabb">Stephen Crabb</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liam_Fox" title="Liam Fox">Liam Fox</a> and <a href="/wiki/Andrea_Leadsom" title="Andrea Leadsom">Andrea Leadsom</a> confirmed as the official contenders to be his successor with <a href="/wiki/Boris_Johnson" title="Boris Johnson">Boris Johnson</a> ruling himself out of the process.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> After Crabb withdrew, Fox and then Gove were eliminated in successive ballots by Conservative MPs, leaving Leadsom and May as the final candidates to be put before the wider Conservative Party membership.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Leadsom subsequently withdrew from the contest on 11 July.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Theresa_May_.282016.E2.80.932019.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Theresa_May_(2016–2019)">Theresa May (2016–2019)</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Premiership_of_Theresa_May" title="Premiership of Theresa May">Premiership of Theresa May</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Theresa_May_official_portrait.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Theresa_May_official_portrait.jpg/170px-Theresa_May_official_portrait.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="207" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Theresa_May_official_portrait.jpg/255px-Theresa_May_official_portrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Theresa_May_official_portrait.jpg/340px-Theresa_May_official_portrait.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3705" data-file-height="4504" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Theresa_May_official_portrait.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Theresa_May" title="Theresa May">Theresa May</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Prime Minister of the United Kingdom</a> (2016–2019)</div></div></div>
<p>On 11 July 2016, <a href="/wiki/Theresa_May" title="Theresa May">Theresa May</a> became the leader of the Conservative Party with immediate effect following the withdrawal from the leadership election of her sole remaining opponent, <a href="/wiki/Andrea_Leadsom" title="Andrea Leadsom">Andrea Leadsom</a>. Appointed Prime Minister of the United Kingdom on 13 July 2016, May promised social reform and a more centrist political outlook for the Conservative Party and its government.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> In a speech after her appointment, May emphasised the term Unionist in the name of the party, reminding all of "the precious, precious bond between England, Scotland, Wales and Northern Ireland".<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> May considers herself a <a href="/wiki/One-nation_conservatism" title="One-nation conservatism">one nation conservative</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>May's early cabinet appointments were interpreted both as "centrist and conciliatory", an effort to reunite the party in the wake of the UK's <a href="/wiki/2016_United_Kingdom_European_Union_membership_referendum" title="2016 United Kingdom European Union membership referendum">vote to leave the European Union</a>, and as "a shift to the right" according to <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>May appointed former Mayor of London <a href="/wiki/Boris_Johnson" title="Boris Johnson">Boris Johnson</a> as <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Foreign_and_Commonwealth_Affairs" title="Secretary of State for Foreign and Commonwealth Affairs">Foreign Secretary</a>, former <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Energy_and_Climate_Change" title="Secretary of State for Energy and Climate Change">Secretary of State for Energy and Climate Change</a> <a href="/wiki/Amber_Rudd" title="Amber Rudd">Amber Rudd</a> as <a href="/wiki/Home_Secretary" title="Home Secretary">Home Secretary</a>, and former <a href="/wiki/Shadow_Home_Secretary" title="Shadow Home Secretary">Shadow Home Secretary</a> <a href="/wiki/David_Davis_(British_politician)" title="David Davis (British politician)">David Davis</a> to the newly created office of <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Exiting_the_European_Union" title="Secretary of State for Exiting the European Union">Brexit Secretary</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Liam_Fox" title="Liam Fox">Liam Fox</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philip_Hammond" title="Philip Hammond">Philip Hammond</a>, who had both previously served as <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Defence" title="Secretary of State for Defence">Secretary of State for Defence</a> (Fox from 2010 to 2011 and Hammond from 2011 to 2014), were appointed to the newly created office of <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_International_Trade" title="Secretary of State for International Trade">International Trade Secretary</a> and as <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">Chancellor of the Exchequer</a> respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
Replacing <a href="/wiki/Michael_Gove" title="Michael Gove">Michael Gove</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Truss" class="mw-redirect" title="Elizabeth Truss">Elizabeth Truss</a> was made Justice Secretary, the "first female Lord Chancellor in the thousand-year history of the role".<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/Andrea_Leadsom" title="Andrea Leadsom">Andrea Leadsom</a>, who was energy minister and May's primary competitor for party leader, was made the new environment secretary.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> However, former Northern Ireland Secretary <a href="/wiki/Theresa_Villiers" title="Theresa Villiers">Theresa Villiers</a> resigned from Cabinet after May offered her a different, non-Cabinet post that was, she said, "not one which I felt I could take on".<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Nearly half of the first May <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Cabinet of the United Kingdom">ministry</a> were women.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In her first speech, May made a promise to combat the "burning injustice" in British society and create a union "between all of our citizens" and promising to be an advocate for the "ordinary working-class family" and not just for "privileged few" in the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In April 2017, the Cabinet agreed to hold a <a href="/wiki/2017_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="2017 United Kingdom general election">general election</a> on 8 June.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> During the resulting campaign, Theresa May asked the electorate to "strengthen my hand" in <a href="/wiki/Brexit" title="Brexit">Brexit</a> negotiations, promised "strong and stable leadership in the national interest" and warned of a "coalition of chaos" under Jeremy Corbyn.
</p><p>Contrary to opinion polling at the time, the election resulted in a <a href="/wiki/Hung_parliament" title="Hung parliament">hung parliament</a>, with the Conservative Party having 317 seats in the House of Commons, but without an overall majority. The <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Unionist_Party" title="Democratic Unionist Party">Democratic Unionist Party</a> suggested it would be able to provide a <a href="/wiki/Conservative%E2%80%93DUP_agreement" title="Conservative–DUP agreement">confidence and supply</a> arrangement depending on negotiations.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> On 9 June 2017, May announced her intention to form a new <a href="/wiki/Second_May_ministry" title="Second May ministry">minority government</a> with support from the DUP,<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> which was finalised on 26 June.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 8 January 2018, May announced her first major cabinet reshuffle, keeping in place most ministers, but promoting others.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In February 2019, three Conservative MPs – <a href="/wiki/Heidi_Allen" title="Heidi Allen">Heidi Allen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Wollaston" title="Sarah Wollaston">Sarah Wollaston</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Anna_Soubry" title="Anna Soubry">Anna Soubry</a> – defected from the party to join <a href="/wiki/The_Independent_Group" class="mw-redirect" title="The Independent Group">The Independent Group</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Pro-Europeanism" title="Pro-Europeanism">pro-EU</a> political association of <a href="/wiki/Member_of_Parliament_(United_Kingdom)" title="Member of Parliament (United Kingdom)">MPs</a> founded by seven former members of the Labour party. The MPs said the reasons for their departure were their opposition to the party's handling of Brexit, what they saw as the takeover of the Conservative party by 'right wing, ... hard-line anti-EU' MPs, and lack of concern from the Conservative party for the 'most vulnerable in society'.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>May announced her resignation from the leadership of the Conservative party on 24 May 2019, intending to leave the role on 7 June. However, she has remained Prime Minister until a successor was elected by the party.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Theresa May resigned as Prime Minister on the 24 July 2019 after her successor, <a href="/wiki/Boris_Johnson" title="Boris Johnson">Boris Johnson</a>, was elected on 23 July 2019. She will remain as the Member of Parliament for the Parliamentary Constituency of <a href="/wiki/Maidenhead" title="Maidenhead">Maidenhead</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Islamophobia">Islamophobia</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Islamophobia_in_the_UK_Conservative_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamophobia in the UK Conservative Party">Islamophobia in the UK Conservative Party</a></div>
<p>In May 2018, the Conservative Party was accused of failing to take action on <a href="/wiki/Islamophobia" title="Islamophobia">Islamophobia</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Muslim_Council_of_Britain" title="Muslim Council of Britain">Muslim Council of Britain</a> has repeatedly demanded an investigation, saying there are now "more than weekly incidents" of Islamophobia involving Conservative candidates and representatives.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc1_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc1-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> In an open letter, it tells chairman <a href="/wiki/Brandon_Lewis" title="Brandon Lewis">Brandon Lewis</a> he must "ensure racists and bigots have no place" in the party.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc1_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc1-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Former party co-chair <a href="/wiki/Sayeeda_Warsi,_Baroness_Warsi" title="Sayeeda Warsi, Baroness Warsi">Sayeeda Baroness Warsi</a> also called on May to publicly acknowledge that Islamophobia is a problem in the Conservative Party, arguing that she has been trying to get the party to address  the issue for over two years.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc1_108-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc1-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-theguardian1_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-theguardian1-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-businessinsider_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-businessinsider-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-independent2_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-independent2-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Muslim_Forum" title="Conservative Muslim Forum">Conservative Muslim Forum</a> also accused the Conservative party of a failure to take action on Islamophobia and joined calls for an independent inquiry.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc2_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc2-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> In addition, 350 mosques and 11 umbrella organisations across the UK have urged the Conservatives to launch internal inquiry into Islamophobia claims.<sup id="cite_ref-independent1_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-independent1-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> In July 2018, the MCB repeated its call for an independent inquiry and accused the Conservatives of turning blind eye to Islamophobia claims.<sup id="cite_ref-theguardian2_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-theguardian2-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> A week later Warsi supported this call, and warned the Conservatives were pursuing a politically damaging policy of denial about the problem in its own ranks. She accused Conservative chair <a href="/wiki/Brandon_Lewis" title="Brandon Lewis">Brandon Lewis</a> of a "woefully inept" response to recent complaints and added that MP <a href="/wiki/Zac_Goldsmith" title="Zac Goldsmith">Zac Goldsmith</a> should receive "mandatory diversity training" for alleged Islamophobia in his <a href="/wiki/2016_London_mayoral_election" title="2016 London mayoral election">failed 2016 London mayoral campaign</a> against <a href="/wiki/Sadiq_Khan" title="Sadiq Khan">Sadiq Khan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-theguardian3_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-theguardian3-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> In August 2018, following comments made by <a href="/wiki/Boris_Johnson" title="Boris Johnson">Boris Johnson</a> regarding the <a href="/wiki/Burqa" title="Burqa">burqa</a>/<a href="/wiki/Niqab" class="mw-redirect" title="Niqab">niqab</a>, the MCB called the comments "particularly regrettable in this current climate" and reiterated its calls for an inquiry, accusing Johnson of "pandering to the far-right".<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> In October 2018, recently selected London Mayoral candidate Shaun Bailey was accused of <a href="/wiki/Islamophobia" title="Islamophobia">Islamophobia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hinduphobia" class="mw-redirect" title="Hinduphobia">Hinduphobia</a> after it was reported that had written a pamphlet in 2005, entitled <i>No Man’s Land</i>, for the <a href="/wiki/Centre_for_Policy_Studies" title="Centre for Policy Studies">Centre for Policy Studies</a>. In it, Bailey argued that accommodating <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Muslims</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hindus</a> is one factor which "[robs] Britain of its community" and that the collapse of community is turning country into a "crime riddled cess pool". He claimed that <a href="/wiki/South_Asians" class="mw-redirect" title="South Asians">South Asians</a> "bring their culture, their country and any problems they might have, with them" and that this was not a problem within the black community "because we’ve shared a religion and in many cases a language".<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Boris_Johnson_.282019.E2.80.93present.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Boris_Johnson_(2019–present)">Boris Johnson (2019–present)</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Boris_Johnson_FCA.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Boris_Johnson_FCA.jpg/170px-Boris_Johnson_FCA.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="223" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Boris_Johnson_FCA.jpg/255px-Boris_Johnson_FCA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Boris_Johnson_FCA.jpg/340px-Boris_Johnson_FCA.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2503" data-file-height="3288" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Boris_Johnson_FCA.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Boris_Johnson" title="Boris Johnson">Boris Johnson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Prime Minister of the United Kingdom</a> (2019–present)</div></div></div>
<p>On 23 July 2019, former <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Foreign_and_Commonwealth_Affairs" title="Secretary of State for Foreign and Commonwealth Affairs">Foreign Secretary</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mayor_of_London" title="Mayor of London">Mayor of London</a> <a href="/wiki/Boris_Johnson" title="Boris Johnson">Boris Johnson</a> defeated Foreign Secretary <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Hunt" title="Jeremy Hunt">Jeremy Hunt</a>, with 66.4% of the vote in the final ballot of Conservative Party members, to become Leader of the Conservative Party.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc230719_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc230719-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Johnson became Prime Minister on the 24 July 2019 after his predecessor <a href="/wiki/Theresa_May" title="Theresa May">Theresa May</a> resigned the same day.
</p><p>Johnson lost his working majority in the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">House of Commons</a> on the 3 September 2019 when <a href="/wiki/Phillip_Lee_(politician)" title="Phillip Lee (politician)">Phillip Lee</a> crossed the floor during Johnson's speech to join the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats_(UK)" title="Liberal Democrats (UK)">Liberal Democrats</a>, later explaining that he believed the Conservative party had been "infected with the twin diseases of <a href="/wiki/Populism" title="Populism">populism</a> and <a href="/wiki/English_nationalism" title="English nationalism">English nationalism</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-bbc030919_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc030919-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> The same day, the former <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">Chancellor of the Exchequer</a> <a href="/wiki/Philip_Hammond" title="Philip Hammond">Philip Hammond</a> announced that he would "defend his party" against "incomers and <a href="/wiki/Entryism" title="Entryism">entryists</a>", perceived by some as referring to Johnson's adviser <a href="/wiki/Dominic_Cummings" title="Dominic Cummings">Dominic Cummings</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Later that same day, a total of twenty-one Conservative MPs, including former Chancellors of the Exchequer <a href="/wiki/Philip_Hammond" title="Philip Hammond">Philip Hammond</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ken_Clarke" class="mw-redirect" title="Ken Clarke">Ken Clarke</a>, ex-ministers, <a href="/wiki/2019_Conservative_Party_leadership_election#Candidates" title="2019 Conservative Party leadership election">2019 leadership candidates</a> <a href="/wiki/Rory_Stewart" title="Rory Stewart">Rory Stewart</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sam_Gyimah" title="Sam Gyimah">Sam Gyimah</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Soames" title="Nicholas Soames">Nicholas Soames</a>, the grandson of former Conservative Party Leader <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a>, had the Conservative <a href="/wiki/September_2019_suspension_of_rebel_Conservative_MPs" title="September 2019 suspension of rebel Conservative MPs">whip withdrawn</a> after voting with the Opposition to grant the House of Commons control over its order paper, leading to Johnson becoming the first Prime Minister to lose his first Commons vote<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
