<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_policy">Economic policy</span></h3>
<p>The Conservative Party believes that free markets and individual achievement are the primary factors behind economic prosperity.
A leading economic theory advocated by Conservatives is supply side economics, this theory holds that reduced income tax rates increase GDP growth and thereby generate the same or more revenue for the government from the smaller tax on the extra growth. This belief is reflected, in part, by the advocacy of tax cuts. The party has recently focused on the <a href="/wiki/Social_market_economy" title="Social market economy">social market economy</a> in Britain, promoting a free market for competition with social balance to create fairness. This has included curbs on the banking sector, enterprise zones to revive regions in Britain and grand infrastructure projects such as high-speed rail.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>One concrete economic policy of recent years has been opposition to the <a href="/wiki/Euro" title="Euro">European</a> single currency. Anticipating the growing <a href="/wiki/Euroscepticism" title="Euroscepticism">Euroscepticism</a> within his party, <a href="/wiki/John_Major" title="John Major">John Major</a> negotiated a British opt-out from the single currency in the 1992 <a href="/wiki/Maastricht_Treaty" title="Maastricht Treaty">Maastricht Treaty</a>, although several members of Major's cabinet, such as <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Clarke" title="Kenneth Clarke">Kenneth Clarke</a>, were personally supportive of EMU participation. Following Major's resignation after the 1997 defeat, each of the four subsequent Conservative leaders, including <a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a>, have positioned the party firmly against the adoption of the <a href="/wiki/Euro" title="Euro">euro</a>. This policy is broadly popular with the British electorate.
</p><p>Following Labour's victory in the 1997 general election, the Conservative Party opposed Labour's decision to grant the <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_England" title="Bank of England">Bank of England</a> independent control of interest rates—on the grounds that it would be a prelude to the abolition of the <a href="/wiki/Pound_sterling" title="Pound sterling">pound sterling</a> and acceptance of the European single currency, and also expressed concern over the removal of monetary policy from democratic control. However, Bank independence was popular amongst the financial community as it helped to keep inflation low.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> The Conservatives accepted Labour's policy in early 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Since coming to power, the 50% top rate of <a href="/wiki/UK_income_tax" class="mw-redirect" title="UK income tax">income tax</a> has been dropped to 45% in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, the Conservative Party has <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_government_austerity_programme" title="United Kingdom government austerity programme">reduced government spending</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_policy">Social policy</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ScarboroughConservativeClub.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/ScarboroughConservativeClub.jpg/170px-ScarboroughConservativeClub.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="256" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/ScarboroughConservativeClub.jpg/255px-ScarboroughConservativeClub.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/ScarboroughConservativeClub.jpg/340px-ScarboroughConservativeClub.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1168" data-file-height="1760" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ScarboroughConservativeClub.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Scarborough,_North_Yorkshire" title="Scarborough, North Yorkshire">Scarborough</a> Conservative Club</div></div></div>
<p>Since the election of <a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a> as party leader, the Conservative Party has distanced itself from association with <a href="/wiki/Social_conservatism" title="Social conservatism">social conservatism</a>. Socially conservative policies such as tax incentives for married couples and the belief that benefits for those out of work should be reduced may have played a role in the party's electoral decline in the 1990s and early 2000s, and so the party has attempted to seek a new direction. The introduction of equal marriage rights for LGBT+ individuals in 2010 can be said to have represented a shift away from social conservatism, though the extent to which this policy truly represented a more 'liberal' Conservative party has been challenged.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Since 1997, debate has occurred within the party between 'modernisers' such as <a href="/wiki/Alan_Duncan" title="Alan Duncan">Alan Duncan</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> who believe that the Conservatives should modify their public stances on social issues, and 'traditionalists' such as <a href="/wiki/Liam_Fox" title="Liam Fox">Liam Fox</a><sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Owen_Paterson" title="Owen Paterson">Owen Paterson</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> who believe that the party should remain faithful to its traditional conservative platform.  <a href="/wiki/William_Hague" title="William Hague">William Hague</a> and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Howard" title="Michael Howard">Michael Howard</a> campaigned on traditionalist grounds in the 2001 and 2005 general elections respectively, and 2001 also saw the election of traditionalist <a href="/wiki/Iain_Duncan_Smith" title="Iain Duncan Smith">Iain Duncan Smith</a> as party leader. In the current parliament, modernising forces are represented by MPs such as <a href="/wiki/Neil_O%27Brien" title="Neil O&#39;Brien">Neil O'Brien</a>, who has argued that the party needs to renew its policies and image, and is said to be inspired by <a href="/wiki/Emmanuel_Macron" title="Emmanuel Macron">Macron'</a>s centrist politics.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ruth_Davidson" title="Ruth Davidson">Ruth Davidson</a> is also seen as a reforming figure, as is the backbench MP <a href="/wiki/Kemi_Badenoch" title="Kemi Badenoch">Kemi Badenoch</a>. Many of the original 'traditionalists' remain influential, though <a href="/wiki/Iain_Duncan_Smith" title="Iain Duncan Smith">Ian Duncan Smith</a>'s influence in terms of Commons contributions has waned.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Many 'traditionalist' backbenchers such as <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Chope" title="Christopher Chope">Christopher Chope</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Bone" title="Peter Bone">Peter Bone</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Rees-Mogg" title="Jacob Rees-Mogg">Jacob Rees-Mogg</a> command significant media attention for their use of <a href="/wiki/Filibuster" title="Filibuster">fillibustering</a> and frequent interjections, and so remain influential forces in the Commons, though they cannot be taken to represent all 'traditionalist' Conservatives.
</p><p>The party has strongly criticised Labour's "state <a href="/wiki/Multiculturalism" title="Multiculturalism">multiculturalism</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> Shadow Home Secretary Dominic Grieve said in 2008 that state multiculturalism policies had created a "terrible" legacy of "cultural despair" and dislocation, which has encouraged support for "extremists" on both sides of the debate.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> David Cameron responded to Grieve's comments by agreeing that policies of "state multiculturalism" that treat social groups as distinct, for example policies that "treat British Muslims as Muslims, rather than as British citizens", are wrong. However, he expressed support for the premise of multiculturalism on the whole, arguing that it was "absolutely right" to encourage society to integrate more "to build a strong British identity for the future".<sup id="cite_ref-:0_137-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Official statistics showed that EU and non-EU <a href="/wiki/Mass_immigration" class="mw-redirect" title="Mass immigration">mass immigration</a>, together with <a href="/wiki/Asylum_seeker" title="Asylum seeker">asylum seeker</a> applications, all increased substantially during Cameron's term in office.<sup id="cite_ref-reuters_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reuters-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-reuters2_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reuters2-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-independent_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-independent-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> However, this was not solely as a result of intentional government policy – during this period, there were significant refugee flows into the UK and an increased level of asylum applications due to conflict and persecution in a number of other states.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> Some political and media discourses at the time suggested that this increase in immigration and reception of refugees and asylum seekers caused significant strain on other areas of social policy through overburdening the NHS and the welfare state – these discourses were influential, but have not been empirically or decisively proven to be true.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_policy">Foreign policy</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Reagan%27s_-_Thatcher%27s_c50515-16.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Reagan%27s_-_Thatcher%27s_c50515-16.jpg/220px-Reagan%27s_-_Thatcher%27s_c50515-16.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Reagan%27s_-_Thatcher%27s_c50515-16.jpg/330px-Reagan%27s_-_Thatcher%27s_c50515-16.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Reagan%27s_-_Thatcher%27s_c50515-16.jpg/440px-Reagan%27s_-_Thatcher%27s_c50515-16.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="2668" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Reagan%27s_-_Thatcher%27s_c50515-16.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a> (second left), <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a> (far left) and their respective spouses in 1988. Thatcher and Reagan developed a close relationship against the Soviet Union.</div></div></div>
<p>For much of the 20th century, the Conservative Party took a broadly <a href="/wiki/Atlanticist" class="mw-redirect" title="Atlanticist">Atlanticist</a> stance in relations with the United States, favouring close ties with the United States and similarly aligned nations such as Canada, Australia and Japan. The Conservatives have generally favoured a diverse range of international alliances, ranging from the <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Treaty_Organization" class="mw-redirect" title="North Atlantic Treaty Organization">North Atlantic Treaty Organization</a> (NATO) to the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth of Nations</a>.
</p><p>Close <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom%E2%80%93United_States_relations" title="United Kingdom–United States relations">US-British relations</a> have been an element of Conservative foreign policy since World War II. Winston Churchill during his 1951–1955 post-war premiership built up a strong relationship with the <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Presidency of Dwight D. Eisenhower">Eisenhower Administration</a> in the United States. Harold Macmillan demonstrated a similarly close relationship with the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic</a> administration of <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a>. Though the US–British relationship in foreign affairs has often been termed a '<a href="/wiki/Special_Relationship" title="Special Relationship">Special Relationship</a>', a term coined by <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Sir Winston Churchill</a>, this has often been observed most clearly where leaders in each country are of a similar political stripe. The former Prime Minister Margaret Thatcher built a close relationship with the American President <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a> in his opposition to the former Soviet Union, but <a href="/wiki/John_Major" title="John Major">John Major</a> was less successful in his personal contacts with <a href="/wiki/George_H._W._Bush" title="George H. W. Bush">George H. W. Bush</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2007)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Out of power and perceived as largely irrelevant by American politicians, Conservative leaders Hague, Duncan-Smith, and Howard each struggled to forge personal relationships with presidents <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a>. However, the <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican</a> 2008 presidential candidate, <a href="/wiki/John_McCain" title="John McCain">John McCain</a>, spoke at the 2006 Conservative Party Conference.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Conservatives have proposed a Pan-African <a href="/wiki/Free_Trade_Area" class="mw-redirect" title="Free Trade Area">Free Trade Area</a>, which it says could help <a href="/wiki/Entrepreneurship" title="Entrepreneurship">entrepreneurial</a> dynamism of African people.<sup id="cite_ref-cameronbbc_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cameronbbc-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> The Conservatives pledged to increase aid spending to 0.7% of national income by 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-cameronbbc_144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cameronbbc-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> They met this pledge in 2014, when spending on aid reached 0.72% of GDP and the commitment was enshrined in UK law in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a> had sought to distance himself from former US President Bush and his <a href="/wiki/Neoconservative" class="mw-redirect" title="Neoconservative">neoconservative</a> foreign policy, calling for a "rebalancing" of US-UK ties<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> and met <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> during his 2008 European tour. Despite traditional links between the centre-right UK Conservatives and US <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republicans</a>, and between centre-left Labour and the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democrats</a>, London Mayor <a href="/wiki/Boris_Johnson" title="Boris Johnson">Boris Johnson</a>, a Conservative, endorsed <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> in the 2008 election.
</p><p>Beyond relations with the United States, the Commonwealth and the EU, the Conservative Party has generally supported a pro free-trade foreign policy within the mainstream of international affairs. The degree to which Conservative Governments have supported <a href="/wiki/Interventionism_(politics)" title="Interventionism (politics)">interventionist</a> or non-interventionist presidents in the US has often varied with the personal relations between a US President and the British Prime Minister.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Defence">Defence</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</span></h4>
<p>Since the <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">terrorist attacks of 11 September 2001</a>, the Conservative Party has supported the coalition military action in <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>. The Conservative Party believes that success in Afghanistan is defined in terms of the Afghans achieving the capability to maintain their own internal and external security.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> They have repeatedly criticised the former <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Government</a> for failing to equip British Forces adequately in the earlier days on the campaign—especially highlighting the shortage of helicopters for British Forces resulting from Gordon Brown's £1.4bn cut to the helicopter budget in 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Strategic_Defence_and_Security_Review">Strategic Defence and Security Review</span></h4>
<p>The Conservative Party believes that in the 21st century defence and security are interlinked. It has pledged to break away from holding a traditional <a href="/wiki/Strategic_Defence_Review" title="Strategic Defence Review">Strategic Defence Review</a> and have committed to carrying out a more comprehensive <a href="/wiki/Strategic_Defence_and_Security_Review_2010" title="Strategic Defence and Security Review 2010">Strategic Defence and Security Review</a> (SDSR) immediately upon coming into office. This review will include both defence and homeland security related matters. The Labour Government last conducted a review in 1998. To prevent a long gap in the future it also pledged to hold regular defence reviews every 4–5&#160;years, and if necessary will put this requirement into legislation. Party officials claim that the SDSR will be a major improvement, and will ensure that Britain maintains generic and flexible capability to adapt to any changing threats. It will be a cross-departmental review that will begin with foreign policy priorities and will bring together all the levers of domestic national security policy with overseas interests and defence priorities.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As well as an SDSR, the Conservative Party pledged in 2010 to undertake a fundamental and far reaching review of the procurement process and how defence equipment is provided in Britain. It pledged to reform the procurement process, compile a Green Paper on Sovereignty Capability, and publish another Defence Industrial Strategy following on from the Defence Industrial Strategy in 2005. The Conservative Party has said that there will be four aims for British defence procurement: to provide the best possible equipment at the best possible price; to streamline the procurement process to ensure the speedy delivery of equipment to the front line; to support our industry jobs at home by increasing defence exports; to provide defence procurement that underpins strategic relationships abroad and; to provide predictability to the defence industry.
</p><p>The Conservative Party also pledged to increase Britain's share of the global defence market as Government policy.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe_and_NATO">Europe and NATO</span></h4>
<p>The Conservative Party aims to build enhanced bilateral defence relations with key European partners and believes that it is in Britain's national interest to cooperate fully with all its European neighbours. It has pledged to ensure that any EU military capability must supplement and not supplant British national defence and <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a>, and that it is not in the British interest to hand over security to any supranational body.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Conservatives see it as a priority to encourage all members of the European Union to do more in terms of a commitment to European security at home and abroad.
</p><p>Regarding the defence role of the European Union, the Conservatives pledged to re-examine some of Britain's EU Defence commitments to determine their practicality and utility; specifically, to reassess UK participation provisions like Permanent Structured Cooperation, the <a href="/wiki/European_Defence_Agency" title="European Defence Agency">European Defence Agency</a> and EU Battlegroups to determine if there is any value in Britain's participation.
</p><p>The Conservative Party upholds the view that NATO should remain the most important security alliance for the United Kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> It believes that NATO, which has been the cornerstone of British security for the past 60&#160;years, should continue to have primacy on all issues relating to Europe's defence, and pledged in 2010 to make NATO reform a key strategic priority.
</p><p>It has also called on the so-called fighting/funding gap to be changed and have called on the creation of a fairer funding mechanism for NATO's expeditionary operations. As well as this, the Conservatives believe that there is scope for expanding NATO's Article V to include new 21st Century threats such as energy and cyber security.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_weapons">Nuclear weapons</span></h4>
<p>The 2010 manifesto said the Conservatives will maintain Britain's continuous at sea, independent, submarine based strategic nuclear deterrent based on the <a href="/wiki/Trident_nuclear_programme" class="mw-redirect" title="Trident nuclear programme">Trident missile system</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_150-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health_policy">Health policy</span></h3>
<p>In 1945, the Conservatives first declared support for universal healthcare.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> Since entering office in 2010, they have introduced the <a href="/wiki/Health_and_Social_Care_Act_2012" title="Health and Social Care Act 2012">Health and Social Care Act</a>, constituting the biggest reformation that the NHS has ever undertaken. However, there has been much criticism and protest about the 2010 government's actions on the NHS, focussing on budget cuts and privatisation of services. After a 2013 <a href="/wiki/Trades_union" class="mw-redirect" title="Trades union">union</a> protest said by police to have been one of the largest protests seen in Manchester, the general secretary of the <a href="/wiki/Trades_Union_Congress" title="Trades Union Congress">Trades Union Congress</a> (TUC) said that austerity was having a devastating effect, with 21,000 NHS jobs lost over the previous three months alone, and that "The NHS is one of Britain's finest achievements and we will not allow ministers to destroy, through cuts and privatisation, what has taken generations to build." The Department of Health responded that there was "absolutely no government policy to privatise NHS services".<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Drug_policies">Drug policies</span></h3>
<p>Views on drug legality and policing vary greatly within the Conservative Party. Some Conservative politicians such as <a href="/wiki/Alan_Duncan" title="Alan Duncan">Alan Duncan</a> take the <a href="/wiki/Libertarianism" title="Libertarianism">libertarian</a> approach that individual freedom and economic freedom of industry and trade should be respected. Other Conservative politicians, despite being <a href="/wiki/Economic_liberalism" title="Economic liberalism">economically liberal</a>, are in favour of full <a href="/wiki/Prohibition_of_drugs" title="Prohibition of drugs">prohibition of the ownership and trade of many drugs</a>. Other Conservatives are in the middle ground, favouring stances such as looser regulation and <a href="/wiki/Drug_decriminalization" class="mw-redirect" title="Drug decriminalization">decriminalisation</a> of some drugs. Legalisation of <a href="/wiki/Medical_cannabis" title="Medical cannabis">cannabis for medical uses</a> is favoured by some Conservative politicians, including <a href="/wiki/Boris_Johnson" title="Boris Johnson">Boris Johnson</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education_and_research">Education and research</span></h3>
<p>In education, the Conservatives have pledged to review the <a href="/wiki/National_Curriculum_(England,_Wales_and_Northern_Ireland)" class="mw-redirect" title="National Curriculum (England, Wales and Northern Ireland)">National Curriculum</a>, and introduce the <a href="/wiki/General_Certificate_of_Secondary_Education#English_Baccalaureate" title="General Certificate of Secondary Education">English Baccalaureate</a>. The restoration of discipline was also highlighted, as they want it to be easier for pupils to be searched for contraband items, the granting of anonymity to teachers accused by pupils, and the banning of expelled pupils being returned to schools via appeal panels.
</p><p>In Higher education, the Conservatives have increased tuition fees to £9,250 per year, however have ensured that this will not be paid by anyone until they are earning over £25,000. The Scottish Conservatives also support the re-introduction of tuition fees in Scotland. In 2016 the Conservative government extended student loan access in England to postgraduate students to help improve access to education.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Within the EU, the UK is one of the largest recipients of research funding in the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, receiving £7 billion between 2007 and 2015, which is invested in universities and research-intensive businesses.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> Following the vote to leave the EU, Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Theresa_May" title="Theresa May">Theresa May</a> guaranteed that the Conservative government would protect funding for existing research and development projects in the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Family_policy">Family policy</span></h3>
<p>As prime minister, <a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a> wanted to 'support family life in Britain' and put families at the centre of domestic social policy making.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> He stated in 2014 that there was 'no better place to start' in the Conservative mission of 'building society from the bottom up' than the family, which was responsible for individual welfare and well-being long before the welfare state came into play.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_158-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> He also argued that 'family and politics are inextricably linked'.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_158-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> Both Cameron and <a href="/wiki/Theresa_May" title="Theresa May">Theresa May</a> have aimed at helping families achieve a work-home balance and have previously proposed to offer all parents 12 months parental leave, to be shared by parents as they choose.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> This policy is now in place, offering 50 weeks total parental leave, of which 37 weeks are paid leave, which can be shared between both parents.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> Other policies have included doubling the free hours of childcare for working parents of three and four-year-olds from 15 hours to 30 hours a week during term-time, although parents can reduce the number of hours per week to 22 and spread across 52 weeks of the year. However, numerous childcare providers have argued that this policy is unworkable, as it means that they don't receive enough compensation from the government to make up for the lost childcare fees, and so their businesses are no longer financially viable.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> The government also introduced a policy to fund 15 hours a week of free education and childcare for 2-year olds in England if parents are receiving certain state benefits or the child has a SEN statement or diagnosis, worth £2,500 a year per child.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jobs_and_welfare_policy">Jobs and welfare policy</span></h3>
<p>One of the Conservatives' key policy goals in 2010 was to reduce the number of people in the UK claiming state benefits, and increase the number of people in the workforce. Between 2010 and 2014, all claimants of <a href="/wiki/Incapacity_Benefit" title="Incapacity Benefit">Incapacity Benefit</a> were moved onto a new benefit scheme, <a href="/wiki/Employment_and_Support_Allowance" title="Employment and Support Allowance">Employment and Support Allowance</a>, which was then subsumed into the <a href="/wiki/Universal_Credit" title="Universal Credit">Universal Credit</a> system alongside other welfare benefits in 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> The Universal Credit system has come under immense scrutiny since its introduction. Shortly after her appointment to the <a href="/wiki/Department_for_Work_and_Pensions" title="Department for Work and Pensions">Department for Work and Pensions</a>, Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/Amber_Rudd" title="Amber Rudd">Amber Rudd</a> acknowledged there were 'real problems' with the Universal Credit system, especially the wait times for initial payments and the housing payments aspect of the combined benefits.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> Rudd pledged specifically to review and address the uneven impact of Universal Credit implementation on economically disadvantaged women, which had been the subject of numerous reports by the Radio 4 <a href="/wiki/You_and_Yours" title="You and Yours">You and Yours</a> programme and others.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_166-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Until 1999, Conservatives opposed the creation of a <a href="/wiki/National_Minimum_Wage_Act_1998" title="National Minimum Wage Act 1998">national minimum wage</a>, as they believed it would cost jobs, and businesses would be reluctant to start business in the UK from fear of high labour costs.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> However the party have since pledged support and in the <a href="/wiki/July_2015_United_Kingdom_budget" title="July 2015 United Kingdom budget">July 2015 budget</a>, Chancellor George Osborne announced a <a href="/wiki/National_Living_Wage" title="National Living Wage">National Living Wage</a> of £9/hour, to be introduced by 2020, for those aged 25 and over.<sup id="cite_ref-measures_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-measures-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> The National Minimum Wage in 2012 was £6.19 for over-21 year olds, so the proposed rises in National Living Wage by 2020 will represent a significantly higher pay for many.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> However, the National Living Wage varies significantly by age, and there is evidence that up to 200,000 eligible individuals are not actually receiving the pay that they should be under the National Living Wage scheme.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> The party support, and have implemented, the restoration of the link between pensions and earnings, and seek to raise retirement age from 65 to 67 by 2028.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_and_climate_change_policy">Energy and climate change policy</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a> brought several '<a href="/wiki/Green_conservatism" title="Green conservatism">green</a>' issues to the forefront of his 2010 campaign. These included proposals designed to impose a tax on workplace car parking spaces, a halt to airport growth, a tax on cars with exceptionally poor petrol mileage, and restrictions on car advertising. Many of these policies were implemented in the Coalition—including the '<a href="/wiki/Green_Deal" class="mw-redirect" title="Green Deal">Green Deal</a>'.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Justice_and_crime_policy">Justice and crime policy</span></h3>
<p>In 2010, the Conservatives campaigned with the conviction to cut the perceived bureaucracy of the modern police force and pledged greater legal protection to people convicted of defending themselves against intruders. They also supported the creation of a UK Bill of Rights to replace the <a href="/wiki/Human_Rights_Act_1998" title="Human Rights Act 1998">Human Rights Act 1998</a>, but this was vetoed by their coalition partners the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats_(UK)" title="Liberal Democrats (UK)">Liberal Democrats</a>. Some Conservatives, particularly within the socially conservative <a href="/wiki/Cornerstone_Group" title="Cornerstone Group">Cornerstone Group</a>, support the re-introduction of the <a href="/wiki/Capital_punishment_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Capital punishment in the United Kingdom">death penalty</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European_Union_policy">European Union policy</span></h3>
<p>No subject has proved more divisive in the Conservative Party in recent history than the role of the United Kingdom within the European Union. Though the principal architect of the UK's entry into the <a href="/wiki/European_Communities" title="European Communities">European Communities</a> (which became the European Union) was Conservative Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Edward_Heath" title="Edward Heath">Edward Heath</a>, and both <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harold_Macmillan" title="Harold Macmillan">Harold Macmillan</a> favoured some form of European union, the bulk of contemporary Conservative opinion is opposed to closer economic and particularly political union with the EU. This is a noticeable shift in British politics, as in the 1960s and 1970s the Conservatives were more pro-Europe than the Labour Party: for example, in the 1971 House of Commons vote on whether the UK should join the European Economic Community, only 39 of the then 330 Conservative MPs were opposed to membership.<sup id="cite_ref-georgiou_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-georgiou-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> Divisions on Europe came to the fore under the premiership of <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a> (1979–1990) and were cited by several ministers resigning, including <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Howe" title="Geoffrey Howe">Geoffrey Howe</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Deputy_Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Deputy Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Deputy Prime Minister</a>, whose resignation triggered <a href="/wiki/1990_Conservative_Party_leadership_election" title="1990 Conservative Party leadership election">the challenge that ended Thatcher's leadership</a>. Under Thatcher's successor, <a href="/wiki/John_Major" title="John Major">John Major</a> (1990–1997), the slow process of integration within the EU forced party tensions to the surface. A core of <a href="/wiki/Euroscepticism" title="Euroscepticism">Eurosceptic</a> MPs under Major used the small Conservative majority in Parliament to oppose Government policy on the <a href="/wiki/Maastricht_Treaty" title="Maastricht Treaty">Maastricht Treaty</a>. By doing so they undermined Major's ability to govern.
</p><p>The Conservative Party has members with varying opinions of the EU, with <a href="/wiki/Pro-European" class="mw-redirect" title="Pro-European">pro-European</a> Conservatives joining the affiliate <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Europe_Group" class="mw-redirect" title="Conservative Europe Group">Conservative Group for Europe</a>, while some Eurosceptics left the party to join the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Independence_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom Independence Party">United Kingdom Independence Party</a>. Whilst the vast majority of Conservatives in recent decades have been Eurosceptics, views among this group regarding the UK's relationship with the EU have been polarised between moderate, soft Eurosceptics who support continued British membership but oppose further harmonisation of regulations affecting business and accept participation in a <a href="/wiki/Multi-speed_Europe" title="Multi-speed Europe">multi-speed Europe</a>, and a more radical, economically libertarian faction who oppose policy initiatives from Brussels, support the rolling back of integration measures from the Maastricht Treaty onwards, and have become increasingly supportive of a complete withdrawal.<sup id="cite_ref-georgiou_174-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-georgiou-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> Under current EU practices, the degree to which a Conservative Government could implement policy change regarding the EU would depend directly on the willingness of other EU member states to agree to such policies.
</p><p>In 2009 the Conservative Party actively campaigned against the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Lisbon" title="Treaty of Lisbon">Lisbon Treaty</a>, which it believes would give away too much sovereignty to Brussels. <a href="/wiki/Shadow_Cabinet" title="Shadow Cabinet">Shadow</a> Foreign Secretary <a href="/wiki/William_Hague" title="William Hague">William Hague</a> stated that, should the treaty be in force by the time of an incoming Conservative government, he would "not let matters rest there".<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> However, on 14 June 2009 the shadow Business Secretary, <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Clarke" title="Kenneth Clarke">Kenneth Clarke</a>, said in an interview to the <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a> that the Conservative Party would not reopen negotiations on the Lisbon Treaty if the Irish backed it in a new referendum,<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> which they did on 2 October 2009.
</p><p>The Conservative Party pledged an in-out referendum on membership of the European Union after a renegotiation. The <a href="/wiki/2016_United_Kingdom_European_Union_membership_referendum" title="2016 United Kingdom European Union membership referendum">referendum took place on 23 June 2016</a>, and resulted in a vote to leave the European Union – a policy commonly referred to as <a href="/wiki/Brexit" title="Brexit">Brexit</a>. Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Theresa_May" title="Theresa May">Theresa May</a> signed the notice under Article 50 of the Lisbon Treaty which officially began Britain's withdrawal from the EU, on 28 March 2017, and at 12:20 on 29 March 2017, the UK ambassador Tim Barrow delivered the notice to EU president Donald Tusk, officially triggering what was intended to be a two-year process of leaving the European Union.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Union_policy">Union policy</span></h3>
<p>The Conservatives staunchly support the maintenance of the United Kingdom, and oppose the independence of any of the <a href="/wiki/Countries_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Countries of the United Kingdom">countries of the United Kingdom</a>: <a href="/wiki/English_independence" title="English independence">England</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scottish_independence" title="Scottish independence">Scotland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Welsh_independence" title="Welsh independence">Wales</a> or <a href="/wiki/Irish_republicanism" title="Irish republicanism">Northern Ireland</a> from it. They have had a mixed history on support for <a href="/wiki/History_of_Scottish_devolution" title="History of Scottish devolution">Scottish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Welsh_devolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Welsh devolution">Welsh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Devolution_for_Northern_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="Devolution for Northern Ireland">Northern Irish devolution</a>.
</p><p>In 1968, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Heath" title="Edward Heath">Edward Heath</a> issued his '<a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_Perth" title="Declaration of Perth">Perth declaration</a>', in support of a Scottish assembly, in the wake of growing nationalism. However, the cause went unanswered during his turbulent premiership, and under <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Major" title="John Major">John Major</a>'s leadership, the Conservatives vehemently opposed devolution, and campaigned against it in the <a href="/wiki/1997_Scottish_devolution_referendum" title="1997 Scottish devolution referendum">1997 devolution referendum</a>. Following the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Parliament" title="Scottish Parliament">Scottish Parliament</a>'s establishment in 1999, they have vowed to support its continued existence, and along with Labour and the Liberal Democrats, they supported the <a href="/wiki/Scotland_Bill_(2011)" class="mw-redirect" title="Scotland Bill (2011)">Scotland Bill (2011)</a>, granting further devolution of power. They campaigned alongside Labour and the Liberal Democrats against full Scottish Independence in the 2014 <a href="/wiki/2014_Scottish_independence_referendum" title="2014 Scottish independence referendum">Scottish Independence referendum</a>.
</p><p>In Wales, the Conservatives campaigned against devolution in the <a href="/wiki/1997_Welsh_devolution_referendum" title="1997 Welsh devolution referendum">1997 referendum</a>, however likewise as with Scotland, they have vowed to maintain the <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_for_Wales" title="National Assembly for Wales">Welsh Assembly</a>'s continued existence, and in 2011 supported the further devolution of power.
</p><p>In Northern Ireland, the Conservatives suspended the parliament in 1973 in the wake of the growing <a href="/wiki/The_Troubles" title="The Troubles">Troubles</a>, and made unsuccessful attempts to re-establish it in the same year, and in 1982. They supported the <a href="/wiki/Belfast_Agreement" class="mw-redirect" title="Belfast Agreement">Belfast Agreement</a> negotiated by the Blair government in 1998, and in 2009, negotiated an electoral pact with the declining <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Unionist_Party" title="Ulster Unionist Party">Ulster Unionist Party</a>, whom it had previously been allied to before 1973 and informally during the John Major's tenure as Prime Minister. The pact was abandoned for the 2015 general election, where the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Conservatives" title="Northern Ireland Conservatives">Northern Ireland Conservatives</a> ran their own candidates.
</p><p>On 4 October 2016, the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Unionist_Party" title="Democratic Unionist Party">Democratic Unionist Party</a>'s leader <a href="/wiki/Arlene_Foster" title="Arlene Foster">Arlene Foster</a> and MPs held a champagne reception at the Conservative Party <a href="/wiki/Party_conference_season" title="Party conference season">conference</a>, marking what some have described as an "informal coalition" or an "understanding" between the two parties to account for the Conservatives' narrow majority in the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">House of Commons</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> Since then, the DUP has generally supported Conservative legislation.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The party opposed Labour's attempts to <a href="/wiki/2004_Northern_England_devolution_referendums" class="mw-redirect" title="2004 Northern England devolution referendums">devolve power to the northern regions of England</a> in 2004. It declared support for a commission into the <a href="/wiki/West_Lothian_Question" class="mw-redirect" title="West Lothian Question">West Lothian Question</a>, as to whether or not only English MPs should be able to vote on issues solely affecting English matters following the Scottish Independence Referendum.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="British_constitution">British constitution</span></h3>
<p>Traditionally the Conservative Party have been defenders of Britain's unwritten constitution and system of government. The party opposed many of <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a>'s reforms, such as the removal of the hereditary peers,<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> the incorporation of the <a href="/wiki/European_Convention_on_Human_Rights" title="European Convention on Human Rights">European Convention on Human Rights</a> into British law, and the 2009 creation of the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Supreme Court of the United Kingdom">Supreme Court of the United Kingdom</a>, a function formerly <a href="/wiki/Lords_of_Appeal_in_Ordinary" title="Lords of Appeal in Ordinary">carried out by the House of Lords</a>.
</p><p>Until 2001 most members of the party were against an elected <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a>; however opinion was later split, shown in the vote on the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords_Reform_Bill_2012" title="House of Lords Reform Bill 2012">House of Lords Reform Bill 2012</a>, when 80 backbenchers voted for an 80% elected upper chamber, and 110 did not.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There was also a split on whether to introduce a British Bill of Rights that would replace the <a href="/wiki/Human_Rights_Act_1998" title="Human Rights Act 1998">Human Rights Act 1998</a>; David Cameron expressed support, but Ken Clarke described it as "xenophobic and legal nonsense".<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Organisation">Organisation</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Party_structure">Party structure</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Conservative_Party_conference_2011.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Conservative_Party_conference_2011.jpg/220px-Conservative_Party_conference_2011.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Conservative_Party_conference_2011.jpg/330px-Conservative_Party_conference_2011.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Conservative_Party_conference_2011.jpg/440px-Conservative_Party_conference_2011.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="3000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Conservative_Party_conference_2011.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/National_Conservative_Convention" title="National Conservative Convention">National Conservative Convention</a> is held during the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)_Conference" class="mw-redirect" title="Conservative Party (UK) Conference">Conservative Party Conference</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The Conservative Party comprises the voluntary party, parliamentary party (sometimes called the political party) and the professional party.
</p><p>Members of the public join the party by becoming part of a local constituency <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Association" title="Conservative Association">Conservative Association</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Central-Office-v-Burrell_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Central-Office-v-Burrell-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> The country is also divided into regions, with each region containing a number of areas, both having a similar structure to constituency associations. The <a href="/wiki/National_Conservative_Convention" title="National Conservative Convention">National Conservative Convention</a> sets the voluntary party's direction. It is composed of all association chairs, officers from areas and regions, and 42 representatives and the Conservative Women's Organisation.<sup id="cite_ref-organisation_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-organisation-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> The Convention meets twice a year. Its Annual General Meeting is usually held at Spring Forum, with another meeting usually held at the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)_Conference" class="mw-redirect" title="Conservative Party (UK) Conference">Conservative Party Conference</a>. In the organisation of the Conservative Party, constituency associations dominate selection of local candidates, and some associations have organised <a href="/wiki/Open_primary" class="mw-redirect" title="Open primary">open</a> <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)_parliamentary_primaries" title="Conservative Party (UK) parliamentary primaries">parliamentary primaries</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/1922_Committee" title="1922 Committee">1922 Committee</a> consists of <a href="/wiki/Backbench" class="mw-redirect" title="Backbench">backbench</a> MPs, meeting weekly while parliament is sitting. Frontbench MPs have an open invitation to attend. The 1922 Committee plays a crucial role in the selection of party leaders. All Conservative MPs are members of the 1922 Committee by default. There are 20 executive members of the Committee, agreed by consensus among backbench MPs.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Campaign_Headquarters" title="Conservative Campaign Headquarters">Conservative Campaign Headquarters</a> (CCHQ) is effectively head of the Professional Party and leads <a href="/wiki/Political_funding_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Political funding in the United Kingdom">financing</a>, organisation of elections and drafting of policy.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_Board" title="Conservative Party Board">Conservative Party Board</a> is the party's ultimate <a href="/wiki/Decision_making" class="mw-redirect" title="Decision making">decision making</a> body, responsible for all operational matters (including fundraising, membership and candidates) and is made up of representatives from each (voluntary, political and professional) section of the Party.<sup id="cite_ref-organisation_185-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-organisation-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> The Party Board meets about once a month and works closely with CCHQ, elected representatives and the voluntary membership mainly through a number of management sub-committees (such as membership, candidates and conferences).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Membership">Membership</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:UK_popular_vote.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/UK_popular_vote.svg/350px-UK_popular_vote.svg.png" decoding="async" width="350" height="190" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/UK_popular_vote.svg/525px-UK_popular_vote.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/UK_popular_vote.svg/700px-UK_popular_vote.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="543" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:UK_popular_vote.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Share of the vote received by Conservatives (blue), Whigs/Liberals/Liberal Democrats (orange), Labour (red) and others (grey) in general elections since 1832<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Membership peaked in the mid-1950s at approximately 3 million, before declining steadily through the second half of the 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-hoclibrary_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hoclibrary-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> Despite an initial boost shortly after <a href="/wiki/2005_Conservative_Party_leadership_election" title="2005 Conservative Party leadership election">David Cameron's election as leader</a> in December 2005, membership resumed its decline in 2006 to a lower level than when he was elected. In 2010, the Conservative Party had about 177,000 members according to activist <a href="/wiki/Tim_Montgomerie" title="Tim Montgomerie">Tim Montgomerie</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> and in 2013 membership was estimated by the party itself at 134,000.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup>  The membership fee for the Conservative Party is £25, or £5 if the member is under the age of 23. From April 2013 until the 2015 general election people could join <a href="/wiki/Team2015" title="Team2015">Team2015</a> without being Party members, and take part in political campaigning for the party. At the 2018 Conservative Spring Forum, Party Chairman <a href="/wiki/Brandon_Lewis" title="Brandon Lewis">Brandon Lewis</a> announced that the party's membership stood at 124,000.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2013 the Conservative Party lost an estimated 35-40% of its membership due to the Same Sex Marriage Bill.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prospective_parliamentary_candidates">Prospective parliamentary candidates</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Prospective_parliamentary_candidate" title="Prospective parliamentary candidate">Prospective parliamentary candidate</a></div>
<p>Associations select their constituency's candidates.<sup id="cite_ref-Central-Office-v-Burrell_184-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Central-Office-v-Burrell-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-dummies_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dummies-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> Some associations <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)_parliamentary_primaries" title="Conservative Party (UK) parliamentary primaries">have organised open parliamentary primaries</a>. A constituency Association must choose a candidate using the rules approved by, and (in England, Wales and Northern Ireland) from a list established by, the Committee on Candidates of the <a href="/wiki/Board_of_the_Conservative_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="Board of the Conservative Party">Board of the Conservative Party</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> Prospective candidates apply to the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Central_Office" class="mw-redirect" title="Conservative Central Office">Conservative Central Office</a> to be included on the approved list of candidates, some candidates will be given the option of applying for any seat they choose, while others may be restricted to certain constituencies.<sup id="cite_ref-personal-representation_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-personal-representation-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-costly_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-costly-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> A Conservative MP can only be deselected at a special general meeting of the local Conservative association, which can only be organised if backed by a petition of more than fifty members.<sup id="cite_ref-personal-representation_196-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-personal-representation-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Young_Conservatives">Young Conservatives</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Young_Conservatives_(UK)" title="Young Conservatives (UK)">Young Conservatives (UK)</a></div>
<p>From 1998–2015, the Conservative Party maintained a <a href="/wiki/Youth_wing" title="Youth wing">youth wing</a> for members under 30 called <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Future" title="Conservative Future">Conservative Future</a>, with branches at both universities and at parliamentary constituency level. By 2006, the group had become the largest political organisation on British university campuses.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> The organisation was closed in 2015 after allegations that bullying by <a href="/wiki/Mark_Clarke_(politician)" title="Mark Clarke (politician)">Mark Clarke</a> had caused the suicide of Elliot Johnson, a 21-year-old party activist.
</p><p>On 16 March 2018, at the Conservative Spring Forum, a new organisation called <a href="/wiki/Young_Conservatives_(UK)" title="Young Conservatives (UK)">Young Conservatives</a><sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> was launched for Conservative Party members aged under 25.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conferences">Conferences</span></h3>
<p>The major annual party events are the Spring Forum and the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)_Conference" class="mw-redirect" title="Conservative Party (UK) Conference">Conservative Party Conference</a>, which takes place in Autumn in alternately Manchester or Birmingham. This is when the <a href="/wiki/National_Conservative_Convention" title="National Conservative Convention">National Conservative Convention</a> holds meetings.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Funding">Funding</span></h3>
<p>In the first decade of the 21st century, half the party's funding came from a cluster of just fifty "donor groups", and a third of it from only fifteen.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> In the year after the 2010 general election, half the Tories' funding came from the financial sector.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For 2013, the Conservative Party had an income of £25.4 million, of which £749,000 came from membership subscriptions.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2015, according to accounts filed with the <a href="/wiki/Electoral_Commission_(United_Kingdom)" title="Electoral Commission (United Kingdom)">Electoral Commission</a>, the party had an income of about £41.8&#160;million and expenditures of about £41&#160;million.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Construction businesses, including the <a href="/wiki/Wates_Group" title="Wates Group">Wates Group</a> and <a href="/wiki/JCB_(company)" title="JCB (company)">JCB</a>, have also been significant donors to the party, contributing £430,000 and £8.1m respectively between 2007 and 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-TCI-25Aug2017_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TCI-25Aug2017-205">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="International_organisations">International organisations</span></h3>
<p>The Conservative Party is affiliated to—and plays a leading part in—a number of international organisations.  As a global level, the Conservatives are a member of the <a href="/wiki/International_Democrat_Union" title="International Democrat Union">International Democrat Union</a>, which unites right-of-centre parties, including the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_of_Canada" title="Conservative Party of Canada">Conservative Party of Canada</a>, the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican Party</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_of_Australia" title="Liberal Party of Australia">Liberal Party of Australia</a>, the Indian <a href="/wiki/Bharatiya_Janata_Party" title="Bharatiya Janata Party">Bharatiya Janata Party</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Liberty_Korea_Party" title="Liberty Korea Party">Liberty Korea Party</a>.
</p><p>At a European level, the Conservatives are members of the <a href="/wiki/Alliance_of_Conservatives_and_Reformists_in_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Alliance of Conservatives and Reformists in Europe">Alliance of Conservatives and Reformists in Europe</a> (ACRE), which unites centre-right parties in opposition to a federal <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, through which the Conservatives have ties to the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Unionist_Party" title="Ulster Unionist Party">Ulster Unionist Party</a> and the governing parties of Israel and Turkey, <a href="/wiki/Likud" title="Likud">Likud</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Justice_and_Development_Party_(Turkey)" title="Justice and Development Party (Turkey)">Justice and Development Party</a> respectively. In the European Parliament, the Conservative Party's MEPs sit in the <a href="/wiki/European_Conservatives_and_Reformists" title="European Conservatives and Reformists">European Conservatives and Reformists</a> (ECR) group, which is affiliated to the ACRE.  Party leader David Cameron pushed the foundation of the ECR, which was launched in 2009, along with the Czech <a href="/wiki/Civic_Democratic_Party_(Czech_Republic)" title="Civic Democratic Party (Czech Republic)">Civic Democratic Party</a> and the Polish <a href="/wiki/Law_and_Justice" title="Law and Justice">Law and Justice</a>, before which the Conservative Party's MEPs sat in the <a href="/wiki/European_Democrats" title="European Democrats">European Democrats</a>, which had become a subgroup of the <a href="/wiki/European_People%27s_Party_(European_Parliament_group)" class="mw-redirect" title="European People&#39;s Party (European Parliament group)">European People's Party</a> in the 1990s.  Since the <a href="/wiki/2014_European_Parliament_election" title="2014 European Parliament election">2014 European election</a>, the ECR group has been the third-largest group, with the largest members being the Conservatives (nineteen MEPs), Law and Justice (eighteen MEPs), the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Conservative_Reformers" title="Liberal Conservative Reformers">Liberal Conservative Reformers</a> (five MEPs), and the <a href="/wiki/Danish_People%27s_Party" title="Danish People&#39;s Party">Danish People's Party</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Flemish_Alliance" title="New Flemish Alliance">New Flemish Alliance</a> (four MEPs each).
</p><p>As of June 2009, Cameron required a further four partners apart from the Polish and Czech supports to qualify for official <a href="/wiki/Political_groups_of_the_European_Parliament" title="Political groups of the European Parliament">fraction</a> status in the parliament; the rules state that a caucus needs at least 25 <a href="/wiki/Member_of_the_European_Parliament" title="Member of the European Parliament">MEPs</a> from at least seven of the 27 <a href="/wiki/EU_member_states" class="mw-redirect" title="EU member states">EU member states</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-guardian_03062009_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardian_03062009-206">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> In forming the caucus, Cameron is reportedly breaking with two decades of co-operation by the UK's Conservative Party with the mainstream European Christian Democrats and conservatives in the European parliament, the <a href="/wiki/European_People%27s_Party" title="European People&#39;s Party">European People's Party</a> (EPP) on the grounds that it is dominated by European <a href="/wiki/Federalism" title="Federalism">federalists</a> and supporters of the <a href="/wiki/Lisbon_treaty" class="mw-redirect" title="Lisbon treaty">Lisbon treaty</a>, which is opposed by the Tories.<sup id="cite_ref-guardian_03062009_206-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardian_03062009-206">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> EPP leader <a href="/wiki/Wilfried_Martens" title="Wilfried Martens">Wilfried Martens</a>, former <a href="/wiki/Prime_minister_of_Belgium" class="mw-redirect" title="Prime minister of Belgium">prime minister of Belgium</a>, stated "Cameron's campaign has been to take his party back to the centre in every policy area with one major exception: Europe. […] I can't understand his tactics. <a href="/wiki/Angela_Merkel" title="Angela Merkel">Merkel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nicolas_Sarkozy" title="Nicolas Sarkozy">Sarkozy</a> will never accept his Euroscepticism."<sup id="cite_ref-guardian_03062009_206-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardian_03062009-206">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Party_factions">Party factions</span></h2>
<p>The Conservative Party has a variety of internal factions or ideologies, including <a href="/wiki/One-nation_conservatism" title="One-nation conservatism">one-nation conservatism</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-new_identity_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-new_identity-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Liberal_conservatism" title="Liberal conservatism">liberal conservatism</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cornerstone_Group" title="Cornerstone Group">social conservatism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thatcherism" title="Thatcherism">Thatcherism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Traditional_conservatism" class="mw-redirect" title="Traditional conservatism">traditional conservatism</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_neoconservatism" title="British neoconservatism">neoconservatism</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Euroscepticism" title="Euroscepticism">Euroscepticism</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-whitaker_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-whitaker-212">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pro-Europeanism" title="Pro-Europeanism">pro-Europeanism</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-whitaker_212-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-whitaker-212">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Christian_democracy" title="Christian democracy">Christian democracy</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Localism_(politics)" title="Localism (politics)">localism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Green_conservatism" title="Green conservatism">green conservatism</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Traditionalist_Conservatives">Traditionalist Conservatives</span></h3>
<p>This <a href="/wiki/Social_conservatism" title="Social conservatism">socially conservative</a> right-wing grouping is currently associated with the <a href="/wiki/Cornerstone_Group" title="Cornerstone Group">Cornerstone Group</a> (or Faith, Family, Flag), and is the oldest tradition within the Conservative Party, closely associated with <a href="/wiki/High_Toryism" class="mw-redirect" title="High Toryism">High Toryism</a>. The name stems from its support for three British social institutions (though the Church is an English institution): the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Unitary_state" title="Unitary state">unitary British state</a> and the family. To this end, it emphasises the country's <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglican</a> heritage, oppose any transfer of power away from the United Kingdom—either downwards to the nations and regions or upwards to the European Union—and seek to place greater emphasis on traditional family structures to repair what it sees as a broken society in the UK. It is a strong advocate of marriage and believes the Conservative Party should back the institution with tax breaks and have opposed the alleged assaults on both traditional family structures and fatherhood. Most oppose high levels of immigration and support the lowering of the current 24‑week abortion limit. Some members in the past have expressed support for capital punishment. Prominent MPs from this wing of the party include <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Rosindell" title="Andrew Rosindell">Andrew Rosindell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nadine_Dorries" title="Nadine Dorries">Nadine Dorries</a>, Sir <a href="/wiki/Edward_Leigh" title="Edward Leigh">Edward Leigh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Rees-Mogg" title="Jacob Rees-Mogg">Jacob Rees-Mogg</a>—the latter two being prominent Roman Catholics, notable in a faction marked out by its support for the established Church of England. The conservative English philosopher Sir <a href="/wiki/Roger_Scruton" title="Roger Scruton">Roger Scruton</a> is a representative of the intellectual wing of the <a href="/wiki/Traditional_conservatism" class="mw-redirect" title="Traditional conservatism">traditionalist</a> group: his writings rarely touch on economics and instead focus on conservative perspectives concerning political, social, cultural and moral issues.
</p>
